   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Nuclear power plant</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
      Jump to: 
     <a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 
     <a href="#p-search">search</a> 
    </div> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      This article is about electricity generation from nuclear power. For the general topic of nuclear power, see 
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">Nuclear power</a>.
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Kernkraftwerk_Grafenrheinfeld_-_2013.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Kernkraftwerk_Grafenrheinfeld_-_2013.jpg/300px-Kernkraftwerk_Grafenrheinfeld_-_2013.jpg" width="300" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Kernkraftwerk_Grafenrheinfeld_-_2013.jpg/450px-Kernkraftwerk_Grafenrheinfeld_-_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Kernkraftwerk_Grafenrheinfeld_-_2013.jpg/600px-Kernkraftwerk_Grafenrheinfeld_-_2013.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1431"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Kernkraftwerk_Grafenrheinfeld_-_2013.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> A nuclear power plant (
        <a href="/wiki/Grafenrheinfeld_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Grafenrheinfeld Nuclear Power Plant" class="mw-redirect">Grafenrheinfeld Nuclear Power Plant</a>, 
        <a href="/wiki/Grafenrheinfeld" title="Grafenrheinfeld">Grafenrheinfeld</a>, 
        <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, 
        <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>). The nuclear reactor is contained inside the spherical 
        <a href="/wiki/Containment_building" title="Containment building">containment building</a> in the center – left and right are 
        <a href="/wiki/Cooling_tower" title="Cooling tower">cooling towers</a> which are common cooling devices used in all 
        <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power stations</a>, and likewise, emit water vapor from the non-
        <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive" class="mw-redirect">radioactive</a> 
        <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a> section of the power plant.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Bohunice_(13).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Bohunice_%2813%29.jpg/300px-Bohunice_%2813%29.jpg" width="300" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Bohunice_%2813%29.jpg/450px-Bohunice_%2813%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Bohunice_%2813%29.jpg/600px-Bohunice_%2813%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1624" data-file-height="868"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Bohunice_(13).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Nuclear power plant in 
        <a href="/wiki/Jaslovsk%C3%A9_Bohunice" title="Jaslovské Bohunice">Jaslovské Bohunice</a> in 
        <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>A <b>nuclear power plant</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power station</a> in which the heat source is a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a>. As is typical in all conventional thermal power stations the heat is used to generate steam which drives a <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a> connected to an <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">electric generator</a> which produces <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>. As of 23 April 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">IAEA</a> report there are 435&nbsp;nuclear power reactors in operation<sup id="cite_ref-iaea.org_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iaea.org-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> operating in 31&nbsp;countries.<sup id="cite_ref-UIC_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UIC-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> Nuclear power plants are usually considered to be <a href="/wiki/Base_load_power_plant" title="Base load power plant">base load</a> stations, since fuel is a small part of the cost of production.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p></p>

     <p></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Chp_controlroom.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Chp_controlroom.jpg/220px-Chp_controlroom.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Chp_controlroom.jpg/330px-Chp_controlroom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/Chp_controlroom.jpg 2x" data-file-width="420" data-file-height="289"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Chp_controlroom.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The control room at an American nuclear power plant
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       <i>For more history, see <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor#Early_reactors" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_nuclear_power" title="History of nuclear power" class="mw-redirect">nuclear power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission#History" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>.</i>
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>Electricity was generated by a nuclear reactor for the first time ever on September 3, 1948 at the <a href="/wiki/X-10_Graphite_Reactor" title="X-10 Graphite Reactor">X-10 Graphite Reactor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge,_Tennessee" title="Oak Ridge, Tennessee">Oak Ridge, Tennessee</a> in the United States, and was the first nuclear power plant to power a light bulb.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> The second, larger experiment occurred on December 20, 1951 at the <a href="/wiki/EBR-I" title="EBR-I" class="mw-redirect">EBR-I</a> experimental station near <a href="/wiki/Arco,_Idaho" title="Arco, Idaho">Arco, Idaho</a> in the United States. On June 27, 1954, the world's <a href="/wiki/Obninsk_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Obninsk Nuclear Power Plant">first nuclear power plant</a> to generate electricity for a <a href="/wiki/Power_grid" title="Power grid" class="mw-redirect">power grid</a> started operations at the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Obninsk" title="Obninsk">Obninsk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> The world's first full scale power station, <a href="/wiki/Sellafield" title="Sellafield">Calder Hall</a> in England opened on October 17, 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Systems">Systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Boiling_water_reactor_english.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Boiling_water_reactor_english.svg/220px-Boiling_water_reactor_english.svg.png" width="220" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Boiling_water_reactor_english.svg/330px-Boiling_water_reactor_english.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Boiling_water_reactor_english.svg/440px-Boiling_water_reactor_english.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="820" data-file-height="480"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Boiling_water_reactor_english.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> BWR schematic
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:PressurizedWaterReactor.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PressurizedWaterReactor.gif/220px-PressurizedWaterReactor.gif" width="220" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PressurizedWaterReactor.gif/330px-PressurizedWaterReactor.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PressurizedWaterReactor.gif/440px-PressurizedWaterReactor.gif 2x" data-file-width="625" data-file-height="323"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:PressurizedWaterReactor.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Pressurized water reactor
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <dl> 
      <dd>
       <i>This section has recently been translated from the German Wikipedia.</i>
      </dd> 
     </dl> 
     <p>The conversion to electrical energy takes place indirectly, as in conventional thermal power plants. The fission in a nuclear reactor heats the reactor coolant. The coolant may be <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_water_reactor" title="Pressurized water reactor">water</a> or <a href="/wiki/Gas-cooled_reactor" title="Gas-cooled reactor">gas</a> or even <a href="/wiki/Liquid_metal_cooled_reactor" title="Liquid metal cooled reactor">liquid metal</a> depending on the type of reactor. The reactor coolant then goes to a <a href="/wiki/Steam_generator_(nuclear_power)" title="Steam generator (nuclear power)">steam generator</a> and heats water to produce steam. The pressurized steam is then usually fed to a multi-stage <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a>. After the steam turbine has expanded and partially condensed the steam, the remaining vapor is condensed in a condenser. The condenser is a heat exchanger which is connected to a secondary side such as a river or a <a href="/wiki/Cooling_tower" title="Cooling tower">cooling tower</a>. The water is then pumped back into the steam generator and the cycle begins again. The water-steam cycle corresponds to the <a href="/wiki/Rankine_cycle" title="Rankine cycle">Rankine cycle</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_reactors">Nuclear reactors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Nuclear reactors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">Nuclear reactor</a>
     </div> 
     <p>A <b>nuclear reactor</b> is a device to initiate and control a sustained <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a>. The most common use of nuclear reactors is for the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">generation of electric energy</a> and for the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_marine_propulsion" title="Nuclear marine propulsion">propulsion of ships</a>.</p> 
     <p>Nuclear reactors usually rely on uranium to fuel the chain reaction. Uranium is a very heavy metal that is abundant on Earth and is found in sea water as well as most rocks. Naturally occurring uranium is found in two different isotopes: uranium-238 (U-238), accounting for 99.3% and uranium-235 (U-235) accounting for about 0.7%. Isotopes are atoms of the same element with a different number of neutrons. Thus, U-238 has 146 neutrons and U-235 has 143 neutrons. Different isotopes have different behaviors. For instance, U-235 is fissile which means that it is easily split and gives off a lot of energy making it ideal for nuclear energy. On the other hand, U-238 does not have that property despite it being the same element. Different isotopes also have different half-lives. A half-life is the amount of time it takes for half of a sample of a radioactive element to decay. U-238 has a longer half-life than U-235, so it takes longer to decay over time. This also means that U-238 is less radioactive than U-235</p> 
     <p>The nuclear reactor is the heart of the plant. In its central part, the reactor core's heat is generated by controlled nuclear fission. With this heat, a coolant is heated as it is pumped through the reactor and thereby removes the energy from the reactor. Heat from nuclear fission is used to raise steam, which runs through <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">turbines</a>, which in turn powers either ship's propellers or electrical generators.</p> 
     <p>Since nuclear fission creates radioactivity, the reactor core is surrounded by a protective shield. This containment absorbs radiation and prevents <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radioactive material</a> from being released into the environment. In addition, many reactors are equipped with a dome of concrete to protect the reactor against both internal casualties and external impacts.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steam_turbine">Steam turbine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Steam turbine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">Steam turbine</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The purpose of the steam turbine is to convert the heat contained in steam into mechanical energy. The engine house with the steam turbine is usually structurally separated from the main reactor building. It is so aligned to prevent debris from the destruction of a turbine in operation from flying towards the reactor.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>In the case of a pressurized water reactor, the steam turbine is separated from the nuclear system. To detect a leak in the steam generator and thus the passage of radioactive water at an early stage, an activity meter is mounted to track the outlet steam of the steam generator. In contrast, boiling water reactors pass radioactive water through the steam turbine, so the turbine is kept as part of the control area of the nuclear power plant.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Generator">Generator</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Generator">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">Electric generator</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The generator converts kinetic energy supplied by the turbine into electrical energy. Low-pole AC synchronous generators of high rated power are used.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cooling_system">Cooling system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Cooling system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>A cooling system removes heat from the reactor core and transports it to another area of the plant, where the thermal energy can be harnessed to produce electricity or to do other useful work. Typically the hot coolant is used as a heat source for a <a href="/wiki/Boiler" title="Boiler">boiler</a>, and the pressurized steam from that drives one or more <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a> driven <a href="/wiki/Electrical_generator" title="Electrical generator" class="mw-redirect">electrical generators</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HSWCOOLANT_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HSWCOOLANT-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_valves">Safety valves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Safety valves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>In the event of an emergency, safety valves can be used to prevent pipes from bursting or the reactor from exploding. The valves are designed so that they can derive all of the supplied flow rates with little increase in pressure. In the case of the BWR, the steam is directed into the suppression chamber and condenses there. The chambers on a heat exchanger are connected to the intermediate cooling circuit.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feedwater_pump">Feedwater pump</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Feedwater pump">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>The water level in the steam generator and nuclear reactor is controlled using the feedwater system. The feedwater pump has the task of taking the water from the condensate system, increasing the pressure and forcing it into either the steam generators (in the case of a pressurized water reactor) or directly into the reactor (for boiling water reactors).</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergency_power_supply">Emergency power supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Emergency power supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Most nuclear plants require two distinct sources of offsite power feeding station service transformers that are sufficiently separated in the plant's switchyard and can receive power from multiple transmission lines. In addition in some nuclear plants the turbine generator can power the plant's house loads while the plant is online via station service transformers which tap power from the generator output bus bars before they reach the step-up transformer (these plants also have station service transformers that receive offsite power directly from the switch yard). Even with the redundancy of two power sources total loss of offsite power is still possible. Nuclear power plants are equipped with emergency power.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Workers_in_a_nuclear_power_plant">Workers in a nuclear power plant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Workers in a nuclear power plant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <ul> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_engineering" title="Nuclear engineering">Nuclear engineers</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Reactor_operator" title="Reactor operator">Reactor operators</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Health_physics" title="Health physics">Health physicists</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant_emergency_response_team" title="Nuclear power plant emergency response team">Emergency response team personnel</a></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="Nuclear Regulatory Commission">Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a> Resident Inspectors</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>In the United States and Canada, workers except for management, professional (such as engineers) and security personnel are likely to be members of either the <a href="/wiki/International_Brotherhood_of_Electrical_Workers" title="International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers">International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers</a> (IBEW) or the <a href="/wiki/Utility_Workers_Union_of_America" title="Utility Workers Union of America">Utility Workers Union of America</a> (UWUA), or one of the various trades and labor unions representing Machinist, laborers, boilermakers, millwrights, iron workers etc.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants" class="mw-redirect">Economics of new nuclear power plants</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg/220px-Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg/330px-Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg/440px-Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="667"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Bruce-Nuclear-Szmurlo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Bruce Nuclear Generating Station">Bruce Nuclear Generating Station</a>, the 
        <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_power_stations" title="List of nuclear power stations">largest nuclear power facility</a> in the world
        <sup id="cite_ref-Bruce_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bruce-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The economics of new nuclear power plants is a controversial subject, and multibillion-dollar investments ride on the choice of an energy source. Nuclear power plants typically have high capital costs, but low direct fuel costs, with the costs of fuel extraction, processing, use and <a href="/wiki/Price_Anderson_Act" title="Price Anderson Act" class="mw-redirect">spent fuel storage internalized costs</a>. Therefore, comparison with other power generation methods is strongly dependent on assumptions about construction timescales and capital financing for nuclear plants. Cost estimates take into account <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">plant decommissioning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" title="Nuclear waste" class="mw-redirect">nuclear waste</a> storage or recycling costs in the United States due to the <a href="/wiki/Price_Anderson_Act" title="Price Anderson Act" class="mw-redirect">Price Anderson Act</a>. With the prospect that all <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel" title="Spent nuclear fuel">spent nuclear fuel</a>/"nuclear waste" could potentially be recycled by using future reactors, <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">generation IV reactors</a>, that are being designed to completely close the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">nuclear fuel cycle</a>.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Kerncentrale_Doel_in_werking.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Kerncentrale_Doel_in_werking.jpg/220px-Kerncentrale_Doel_in_werking.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Kerncentrale_Doel_in_werking.jpg/330px-Kerncentrale_Doel_in_werking.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Kerncentrale_Doel_in_werking.jpg/440px-Kerncentrale_Doel_in_werking.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1192" data-file-height="800"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Kerncentrale_Doel_in_werking.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Nuclear reactors in operations releasing hot steams as the side products
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>On the other hand, construction, or capital cost aside, measures to <a href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_global_warming" title="Mitigation of global warming" class="mw-redirect">mitigate</a> <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> such as a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tax" title="Carbon tax">carbon tax</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions_trading" title="Carbon emissions trading" class="mw-redirect">carbon emissions trading</a>, increasingly favor the economics of nuclear power. Further efficiencies are hoped to be achieved through more advanced reactor designs, <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">Generation III reactors</a> promise to be at least 17% more fuel efficient, and have lower capital costs, while futuristic <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactors" title="Generation IV reactors" class="mw-redirect">Generation IV reactors</a> promise 10000-30000% greater fuel efficiency and the elimination of nuclear waste.</p> 
     <p>In Eastern Europe, a number of long-established projects are struggling to find finance, notably Belene in Bulgaria and the additional reactors at Cernavoda in Romania, and some potential backers have pulled out.<sup id="cite_ref-kidd2011_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kidd2011-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Where cheap gas is available and its future supply relatively secure, this also poses a major problem for nuclear projects.<sup id="cite_ref-kidd2011_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kidd2011-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Analysis of the economics of nuclear power must take into account who bears the risks of future uncertainties. To date all operating nuclear power plants were developed by <a href="/wiki/Nationalized" title="Nationalized" class="mw-redirect">state-owned</a> or <a href="/wiki/Regulated_market" title="Regulated market">regulated</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_utility" title="Electric utility">utility monopolies</a><sup id="cite_ref-ft-20100912_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ft-20100912-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> where many of the risks associated with construction costs, operating performance, fuel price, and other factors were borne by consumers rather than suppliers. Many countries have now liberalized the <a href="/wiki/Electricity_market" title="Electricity market">electricity market</a> where these risks, and the risk of cheaper competitors emerging before capital costs are recovered, are borne by plant suppliers and operators rather than consumers, which leads to a significantly different evaluation of the economics of new nuclear power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-MIT-2003_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-2003-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Following the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_I_nuclear_accidents" title="Fukushima I nuclear accidents" class="mw-redirect">Fukushima I nuclear accidents</a>, costs are likely to go up for currently operating and new nuclear power plants, due to increased requirements for on-site spent fuel management and elevated design basis threats.<sup id="cite_ref-Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology_2011_xv_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology_2011_xv-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> However many designs, such as the currently under construction AP1000, use <a href="/wiki/Passive_nuclear_safety" title="Passive nuclear safety">passive nuclear safety</a> cooling systems, unlike those of <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_I" title="Fukushima I" class="mw-redirect">Fukushima I</a> which required active cooling systems, this largely eliminates the necessity to spend more on redundant back up safety equipment.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety_and_accidents">Safety and accidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Safety and accidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>In his book, <i><a href="/wiki/Normal_accidents" title="Normal accidents" class="mw-redirect">Normal accidents</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Perrow" title="Charles Perrow">Charles Perrow</a> says that multiple and unexpected failures are built into society's complex and tightly-coupled nuclear reactor systems. Such accidents are unavoidable and cannot be designed around.<sup id="cite_ref-rev_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rev-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> An interdisciplinary team from MIT has estimated that given the expected growth of nuclear power from 2005 – 2055, at least four serious nuclear accidents would be expected in that period.<sup id="cite_ref-sovsing_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sovsing-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> However the MIT study does not take into account improvements in safety since 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-uvm.edu_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uvm.edu-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology_2003_49_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology_2003_49-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> To date, there have been five serious accidents (<a href="/wiki/Core_damage" title="Core damage" class="mw-redirect">core damage</a>) in the world since 1970 (one at <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island</a> in 1979; one at <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl</a> in 1986; and three at <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima-Daiichi</a> in 2011), corresponding to the beginning of the operation of <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">generation II reactors</a>. This leads to on average one serious accident happening every eight years worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-DIAZMAURIN2011_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DIAZMAURIN2011-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complexity">Complexity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Nuclear_power_plant_safety&amp;action=edit&amp;section=T-1" title="Template:Nuclear power plant safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Nuclear power plants are some of the most sophisticated and complex energy systems ever designed.<sup id="cite_ref-stor_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stor-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> Any complex system, no matter how well it is designed and engineered, cannot be deemed failure-proof.<sup id="cite_ref-DIAZMAURIN2011_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DIAZMAURIN2011-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Veteran journalist and author <a href="/wiki/Stephanie_Cooke" title="Stephanie Cooke">Stephanie Cooke</a> has argued:</p> 
     <blockquote> 
      <p>The reactors themselves were enormously complex machines with an incalculable number of things that could go wrong. When that happened at <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island</a> in 1979, another fault line in the nuclear world was exposed. One malfunction led to another, and then to a series of others, until the core of the reactor itself began to melt, and even the world's most highly trained nuclear engineers did not know how to respond. The accident revealed serious deficiencies in a system that was meant to protect public health and safety.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     </blockquote> 
     <p>The 1979 <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a> inspired Perrow's book <i><a href="/wiki/Normal_Accidents" title="Normal Accidents">Normal Accidents</a></i>, where a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_accident" title="Nuclear accident" class="mw-redirect">nuclear accident</a> occurs, resulting from an unanticipated interaction of multiple failures in a complex system. TMI was an example of a normal accident because it was "unexpected, incomprehensible, uncontrollable and unavoidable".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <blockquote> 
      <p>Perrow concluded that the failure at Three Mile Island was a consequence of the system's immense complexity. Such modern high-risk systems, he realized, were prone to failures however well they were managed. It was inevitable that they would eventually suffer what he termed a 'normal accident'. Therefore, he suggested, we might do better to contemplate a radical redesign, or if that was not possible, to abandon such technology entirely.<sup id="cite_ref-nature477_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nature477-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> .</p> 
     </blockquote> 
     <p>A fundamental issue contributing to a nuclear power system's complexity is its extremely long lifetime. The timeframe from the start of construction of a commercial nuclear power station through the safe disposal of its last radioactive waste, may be 100 to 150 years.<sup id="cite_ref-stor_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stor-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Failure_modes_of_nuclear_power_plants">Failure modes of nuclear power plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Nuclear_power_plant_safety&amp;action=edit&amp;section=T-2" title="Template:Nuclear power plant safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>There are concerns that a combination of human and mechanical error at a nuclear facility could result in significant harm to people and the environment:<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <blockquote> 
      <p>Operating nuclear reactors contain large amounts of radioactive fission products which, if dispersed, can pose a direct radiation hazard, contaminate soil and vegetation, and be ingested by humans and animals. Human exposure at high enough levels can cause both short-term illness and death and longer-term death by cancer and other diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     </blockquote> 
     <p>It is impossible for a commercial nuclear reactor to explode like a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_bomb" title="Nuclear bomb" class="mw-redirect">nuclear bomb</a> since the fuel is never sufficiently enriched for this to occur.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Nuclear reactors can fail in a variety of ways. Should the instability of the nuclear material generate unexpected behavior, it may result in an uncontrolled power excursion. Normally, the cooling system in a reactor is designed to be able to handle the excess heat this causes; however, should the reactor also experience a <a href="/wiki/Loss_of_coolant" title="Loss of coolant" class="mw-redirect">loss-of-coolant accident</a>, then the fuel may melt or cause the vessel in which it is contained to overheat and melt. This event is called a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_meltdown" title="Nuclear meltdown">nuclear meltdown</a>.</p> 
     <p>After shutting down, for some time the reactor still needs external energy to power its cooling systems. Normally this energy is provided by the power grid to which that plant is connected, or by emergency diesel generators. Failure to provide power for the cooling systems, as happened in <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Fukushima_I_nuclear_accidents" title="Timeline of the Fukushima I nuclear accidents" class="mw-redirect">Fukushima I</a>, can cause serious accidents.</p> 
     <p>Nuclear safety rules in the United States "do not adequately weigh the risk of a single event that would knock out electricity from the grid and from emergency generators, as a quake and tsunami recently did in Japan", Nuclear Regulatory Commission officials said in June 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vulnerability_of_nuclear_plants_to_attack">Vulnerability of nuclear plants to attack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Nuclear_power_plant_safety&amp;action=edit&amp;section=T-3" title="Template:Nuclear power plant safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Nuclear reactors become preferred targets during military conflict and, over the past three decades, have been repeatedly attacked during military air strikes, occupations, invasions and campaigns:<sup id="cite_ref-benj_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-benj-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>In September 1980, Iran bombed the Al Tuwaitha nuclear complex in Iraq in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Scorch_Sword" title="Operation Scorch Sword">Operation Scorch Sword</a>.</li> 
      <li>In June 1981, an Israeli air strike completely destroyed Iraq's Osirak nuclear research facility in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Opera" title="Operation Opera">Operation Opera</a>.</li> 
      <li>Between 1984 and 1987, Iraq bombed Iran's Bushehr nuclear plant six times.</li> 
      <li>On 8 January 1982, Umkhonto we Sizwe, the armed wing of the ANC, attacked South Africa's Koeberg nuclear power plant while it was still under construction.</li> 
      <li>In 1991, the U.S. bombed three nuclear reactors and an enrichment pilot facility in Iraq.</li> 
      <li>In 1991, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War#Iraqi_missile_strikes_on_Israel_and_Saudi_Arabia" title="Gulf War">Iraq launched</a> Scud missiles at Israel's <a href="/wiki/Negev_Nuclear_Research_Center" title="Negev Nuclear Research Center">Dimona nuclear</a> power plant</li> 
      <li>In September 2007, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Orchard" title="Operation Orchard">Israel bombed</a> a Syrian reactor under construction.<sup id="cite_ref-benj_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-benj-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>In the U.S., plants are surrounded by a double row of tall fences which are electronically monitored. The plant grounds are patrolled by a sizeable force of armed guards.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> The NRC's "Design Basis Threat" criterion for plants is a secret, and so what size of attacking force the plants are able to protect against is unknown. However, to <a href="/wiki/Scram" title="Scram">scram</a> (make an emergency shutdown) a plant takes fewer than 5 seconds while unimpeded restart takes hours, severely hampering a terrorist force in a goal to release radioactivity.</p> 
     <p>Attack from the air is an issue that has been highlighted since the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> in the U.S. However, it was in 1972 when three hijackers <a href="/wiki/Southern_Airways_Flight_49" title="Southern Airways Flight 49">took control</a> of a domestic passenger flight along the east coast of the U.S. and threatened to crash the plane into a U.S. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" title="Nuclear weapons" class="mw-redirect">nuclear weapons</a> plant in Oak Ridge, Tennessee. The plane got as close as 8,000 feet above the site before the hijackers’ demands were met.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The most important barrier against the release of radioactivity in the event of an aircraft strike on a nuclear power plant is the containment building and its missile shield. Current NRC Chairman Dale Klein has said "Nuclear power plants are inherently robust structures that our studies show provide adequate protection in a hypothetical attack by an airplane. The NRC has also taken actions that require nuclear power plant operators to be able to manage large fires or explosions—no matter what has caused them."<sup id="cite_ref-air_attack_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-air_attack-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In addition, supporters point to large studies carried out by the U.S. Electric Power Research Institute that tested the robustness of both reactor and waste fuel storage and found that they should be able to sustain a terrorist attack comparable to the <a href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" title="September 11, 2001 attacks" class="mw-redirect">September 11 terrorist attacks</a> in the U.S. Spent fuel is usually housed inside the plant's "protected zone"<sup id="cite_ref-wna-tnfc_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wna-tnfc-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> or a <a href="/wiki/Spent_nuclear_fuel_shipping_cask" title="Spent nuclear fuel shipping cask" class="mw-redirect">spent nuclear fuel shipping cask</a>; stealing it for use in a "<a href="/wiki/Dirty_bomb" title="Dirty bomb">dirty bomb</a>" would be extremely difficult. Exposure to the intense radiation would almost certainly quickly incapacitate or kill anyone who attempts to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-tbotas-dbdj_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tbotas-dbdj-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plant_location">Plant location</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Nuclear_power_plant_safety&amp;action=edit&amp;section=T-4" title="Template:Nuclear power plant safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Corp_of_Eng._6-16-11A_267.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Corp_of_Eng._6-16-11A_267.JPG/220px-Corp_of_Eng._6-16-11A_267.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Corp_of_Eng._6-16-11A_267.JPG/330px-Corp_of_Eng._6-16-11A_267.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Corp_of_Eng._6-16-11A_267.JPG/440px-Corp_of_Eng._6-16-11A_267.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Corp_of_Eng._6-16-11A_267.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Fort_Calhoun_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Fort Calhoun Nuclear Generating Station">Fort Calhoun Nuclear Generating Station</a> surrounded by the 
        <a href="/wiki/2011_Missouri_River_Floods" title="2011 Missouri River Floods" class="mw-redirect">2011 Missouri River Floods</a> on June 16, 2011
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Angra1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Angra1.jpg/220px-Angra1.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Angra1.jpg/330px-Angra1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Angra1.jpg/440px-Angra1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3057" data-file-height="2057"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Angra1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Angra_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Angra Nuclear Power Plant">Angra Nuclear Power Plant</a> in 
        <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro_(state)" title="Rio de Janeiro (state)">Rio de Janeiro state</a>, 
        <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In many countries, plants are often located on the coast, in order to provide a ready source of cooling water for the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety_systems#Essential_service_water_system" title="Nuclear safety systems" class="mw-redirect">essential service water system</a>. As a consequence the design needs to take the risk of flooding and <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a> into account. The <a href="/wiki/World_Energy_Council" title="World Energy Council">World Energy Council</a> (WEC) argues disaster risks are changing and increasing the likelihood of disasters such as <a href="/wiki/Earthquakes" title="Earthquakes" class="mw-redirect">earthquakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyclones" title="Cyclones" class="mw-redirect">cyclones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hurricanes" title="Hurricanes" class="mw-redirect">hurricanes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Typhoons" title="Typhoons" class="mw-redirect">typhoons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flooding" title="Flooding" class="mw-redirect">ﬂooding</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> High temperatures, low precipitation levels and severe <a href="/wiki/Droughts" title="Droughts" class="mw-redirect">droughts</a> may lead to fresh water shortages.<sup id="cite_ref-ODI1_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODI1-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Failure to calculate the risk of flooding correctly lead to a <a href="/wiki/International_Nuclear_Event_Scale#Level_2:_Incident" title="International Nuclear Event Scale">Level 2</a> event on the <a href="/wiki/International_Nuclear_Event_Scale" title="International Nuclear Event Scale">International Nuclear Event Scale</a> during the <a href="/wiki/1999_Blayais_Nuclear_Power_Plant_flood" title="1999 Blayais Nuclear Power Plant flood">1999 Blayais Nuclear Power Plant flood</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> while flooding caused by the <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tōhoku earthquake and tsunami">2011 Tōhoku earthquake and tsunami</a> lead to the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_I_nuclear_accidents" title="Fukushima I nuclear accidents" class="mw-redirect">Fukushima I nuclear accidents</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBusinessweek_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBusinessweek-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The design of plants located in <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">seismically</a> active zones also requires the risk of earthquakes and tsunamis to be taken into account. Japan, India, China and the USA are among the countries to have plants in earthquake-prone regions. Damage caused to Japan's <a href="/wiki/Kashiwazaki-Kariwa_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Kashiwazaki-Kariwa Nuclear Power Plant">Kashiwazaki-Kariwa Nuclear Power Plant</a> during the <a href="/wiki/2007_Ch%C5%ABetsu_offshore_earthquake" title="2007 Chūetsu offshore earthquake">2007 Chūetsu offshore earthquake</a><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-xin_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xin-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> underlined concerns expressed by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Japan#Seismicity" title="Nuclear power in Japan">experts in Japan</a> prior to the Fukushima accidents, who have warned of a <i><a href="/wiki/Genpatsu-shinsai" title="Genpatsu-shinsai">genpatsu-shinsai</a></i> (domino-effect nuclear power plant earthquake disaster).<sup id="cite_ref-ishibashi-abstract-2003_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ishibashi-abstract-2003-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiple_reactors">Multiple reactors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Nuclear_power_plant_safety&amp;action=edit&amp;section=T-5" title="Template:Nuclear power plant safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima nuclear disaster" class="mw-redirect">Fukushima nuclear disaster</a> illustrated the dangers of building multiple nuclear reactor units close to one another. Because of the closeness of the reactors, Plant Director Masao Yoshida "was put in the position of trying to cope simultaneously with core meltdowns at three reactors and exposed fuel pools at three units".<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_safety_systems">Nuclear safety systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Nuclear_power_plant_safety&amp;action=edit&amp;section=T-6" title="Template:Nuclear power plant safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety_systems" title="Nuclear safety systems" class="mw-redirect">Nuclear safety systems</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The three primary objectives of nuclear safety systems as defined by the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Regulatory_Commission" title="Nuclear Regulatory Commission">Nuclear Regulatory Commission</a> are to shut down the reactor, maintain it in a shutdown condition, and prevent the release of radioactive material during events and accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> These objectives are accomplished using a variety of equipment, which is part of different systems, of which each performs specific functions.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Routine_emissions_of_radioactive_materials">Routine emissions of radioactive materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Nuclear_power_plant_safety&amp;action=edit&amp;section=T-7" title="Template:Nuclear power plant safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      For the controversial debate on the health effects by the routine emissions, see 
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate#Health_effects_on_population_near_nuclear_power_plants_and_workers" title="Nuclear power debate">Nuclear power debate#Health effects on population near nuclear power plants and workers</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_nuclear_power#Risk_of_cancer" title="Environmental impact of nuclear power">Environmental impact of nuclear power#Risk of cancer</a>.
     </div> 
     <p>During everyday routine operations, emissions of radioactive materials from nuclear plants are released to the outside of the plants although they are quite slight amounts.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NIRS_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIRS-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-radiationanswers_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-radiationanswers-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_nuclear_power#Power_plant_emissions" title="Environmental impact of nuclear power">daily emissions</a> go into the air, water and soil.<sup id="cite_ref-NIRS_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIRS-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-radiationanswers_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-radiationanswers-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>NRC says, "nuclear power plants sometimes release radioactive gases and liquids into the environment under controlled, monitored conditions to ensure that they pose no danger to the public or the environment",<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> and "routine emissions during normal operation of a nuclear power plant are never lethal".<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>According to the United Nations (<a href="/wiki/UNSCEAR" title="UNSCEAR" class="mw-redirect">UNSCEAR</a>), regular nuclear power plant operation including the nuclear fuel cycle amounts to 0.0002 <a href="/wiki/Sievert" title="Sievert">millisieverts</a> (mSv) annually in average public radiation exposure; the legacy of the Chernobyl disaster is 0.002&nbsp;mSv/a as a global average as of a 2008 report; and natural radiation exposure averages 2.4&nbsp;mSv annually although frequently <a href="/wiki/Background_radiation#Natural_background_radiation" title="Background radiation">varying depending on an individual's location</a> from 1 to 13&nbsp;mSv.<sup id="cite_ref-UNSCEAR_GA_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNSCEAR_GA-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese_public_perception_of_nuclear_power_safety">Japanese public perception of nuclear power safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template:Nuclear_power_plant_safety&amp;action=edit&amp;section=T-8" title="Template:Nuclear power plant safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>In March 2012, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Yoshihiko_Noda" title="Yoshihiko Noda">Yoshihiko Noda</a> said that the Japanese government shared the blame for the Fukushima disaster, saying that officials had been blinded by an image of the country's technological infallibility and were "all too steeped in a safety myth."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Japan has been accused by authors such as journalist Yoichi Funabashi of having an "aversion to facing the potential threat of nuclear emergencies." According to him, a national program to develop robots for use in nuclear emergencies was terminated in midstream because it "smacked too much of underlying danger." Though Japan is a major power in robotics, it had none to send in to Fukushima during the disaster. He mentions that Japan's Nuclear Safety Commission stipulated in its safety guidelines for light-water nuclear facilities that "the potential for extended loss of power need not be considered." However, this kind of extended loss of power to the cooling pumps caused the Fukushima meltdown.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p><br></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">Nuclear power debate</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG/220px-View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG/330px-View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG/440px-View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2240" data-file-height="1680"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Chernobyl_taken_from_Pripyat.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The abandoned city of 
        <a href="/wiki/Prypiat,_Ukraine" title="Prypiat, Ukraine" class="mw-redirect">Prypiat, Ukraine</a>, following the 
        <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a>. The Chernobyl nuclear power plant is in the background.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The nuclear power debate is about the controversy<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eleven_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eleven-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> which has surrounded the deployment and use of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear fission reactors</a> to generate <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">nuclear fuel</a> for civilian purposes. The debate about nuclear power peaked during the 1970s and 1980s, when it "reached an intensity unprecedented in the history of technology controversies", in some countries.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Proponents argue that <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">sustainable energy</a> source which reduces <a href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" title="Carbon emissions" class="mw-redirect">carbon emissions</a> and can increase <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">energy security</a> if its use supplants a dependence on imported fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Proponents advance the notion that nuclear power produces virtually no air pollution, in contrast to the chief viable alternative of fossil fuel. Proponents also believe that nuclear power is the only viable course to achieve energy independence for most Western countries. They emphasize that the risks of storing waste are small and can be further reduced by using the latest technology in newer reactors, and the operational safety record in the Western world is excellent when compared to the other major kinds of power plants.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Opponents say that nuclear power poses many threats to people and the environment. These threats include health risks and environmental damage from <a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">uranium mining</a>, processing and transport, the risk of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear weapons proliferation</a> or sabotage, and the unsolved problem of radioactive <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" title="Nuclear waste" class="mw-redirect">nuclear waste</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gierec_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gierec-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-protest_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-protest-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> The environment issue is also regarding discharge of hot water into the sea. The hot water modifies the environmental conditions for the marine flora fauna. They also contend that reactors themselves are enormously complex machines where many things can and do go wrong, and there have been many serious <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_accidents" title="Nuclear accidents" class="mw-redirect">nuclear accidents</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> Critics do not believe that these risks can be reduced through new <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> They argue that when all the energy-intensive stages of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_chain" title="Nuclear fuel chain" class="mw-redirect">nuclear fuel chain</a> are considered, from uranium mining to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">nuclear decommissioning</a>, nuclear power is not a low-carbon electricity source.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-markd_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-markd-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reprocessing">Reprocessing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Reprocessing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reprocessing" title="Nuclear reprocessing">Nuclear reprocessing</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Nuclear reprocessing technology was developed to chemically separate and recover fissionable plutonium from irradiated nuclear fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> Reprocessing serves multiple purposes, whose relative importance has changed over time. Originally reprocessing was used solely to extract plutonium for producing <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>. With the commercialization of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a>, the reprocessed plutonium was recycled back into <a href="/wiki/MOX_nuclear_fuel" title="MOX nuclear fuel" class="mw-redirect">MOX nuclear fuel</a> for <a href="/wiki/Thermal_reactor" title="Thermal reactor" class="mw-redirect">thermal reactors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Reprocessed_uranium" title="Reprocessed uranium">reprocessed uranium</a>, which constitutes the bulk of the spent fuel material, can in principle also be re-used as fuel, but that is only economic when uranium prices are high or disposal is expensive. Finally, the <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">breeder reactor</a> can employ not only the recycled plutonium and uranium in spent fuel, but all the <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a>, closing the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel_cycle" title="Nuclear fuel cycle">nuclear fuel cycle</a> and potentially multiplying the <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> extracted from <a href="/wiki/Natural_uranium" title="Natural uranium">natural uranium</a> by more than 60 times.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Nuclear reprocessing reduces the volume of high-level waste, but by itself does not reduce radioactivity or heat generation and therefore does not eliminate the need for a geological waste repository. Reprocessing has been politically controversial because of the potential to contribute to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a>, the potential vulnerability to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_terrorism" title="Nuclear terrorism">nuclear terrorism</a>, the political challenges of repository siting (a problem that applies equally to direct disposal of spent fuel), and because of its high cost compared to the once-through fuel cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-bas2011_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bas2011-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> In the United States, the Obama administration stepped back from President Bush's plans for commercial-scale reprocessing and reverted to a program focused on reprocessing-related scientific research.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accident_indemnification">Accident indemnification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Accident indemnification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_Civil_Liability_for_Nuclear_Damage" title="Vienna Convention on Civil Liability for Nuclear Damage">Vienna Convention on Civil Liability for Nuclear Damage</a> puts in place an international framework for nuclear liability.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> However states with a majority of the world's nuclear power plants, including the U.S., Russia, China and Japan, are not party to international nuclear liability conventions.</p> 
     <p>In the U.S., insurance for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_accident" title="Nuclear accident" class="mw-redirect">nuclear</a> or radiological incidents is covered (for facilities licensed through 2025) by the <a href="/wiki/Price-Anderson_Nuclear_Industries_Indemnity_Act" title="Price-Anderson Nuclear Industries Indemnity Act" class="mw-redirect">Price-Anderson Nuclear Industries Indemnity Act</a>.</p> 
     <p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Energy_policy_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Energy policy of the United Kingdom">Energy policy of the United Kingdom</a> through its Nuclear Installations Act of 1965, liability is governed for nuclear damage for which a UK nuclear licensee is responsible. The Act requires compensation to be paid for damage up to a limit of £150 million by the liable operator for ten years after the incident. Between ten and thirty years afterwards, the Government meets this obligation. The Government is also liable for additional limited cross-border liability (about £300 million) under international conventions (<a href="/wiki/Paris_Convention_on_Third_Party_Liability_in_the_Field_of_Nuclear_Energy" title="Paris Convention on Third Party Liability in the Field of Nuclear Energy">Paris Convention on Third Party Liability in the Field of Nuclear Energy</a> and Brussels Convention supplementary to the Paris Convention).<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Decommissioning">Decommissioning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Decommissioning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_decommissioning" title="Nuclear decommissioning">Nuclear decommissioning</a>
     </div> 
     <p><b>Nuclear decommissioning</b> is the dismantling of a nuclear power plant and decontamination of the site to a state no longer requiring protection from radiation for the general public. The main difference from the dismantling of other power plants is the presence of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive" class="mw-redirect">radioactive</a> material that requires special precautions.</p> 
     <p>Warranty period of operation of nuclear power plants is 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-sovsekretno.ru_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sovsekretno.ru-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> One from factors <a href="/wiki/Wear" title="Wear">wear</a> is the destruction of the reactors shell under the action of ionizing radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-sovsekretno.ru_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sovsekretno.ru-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Generally speaking, nuclear plants were designed for a life of about 30&nbsp;years.<sup id="cite_ref-world-nuclear1_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-world-nuclear1-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Newer plants are designed for a 40 to 60-year operating life.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>Decommissioning involves many administrative and technical actions. It includes all clean-up of radioactivity and progressive demolition of the plant. Once a facility is decommissioned, there should no longer be any danger of a radioactive accident or to any persons visiting it. After a facility has been completely decommissioned it is released from regulatory control, and the licensee of the plant no longer has responsibility for its nuclear safety.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historic_accidents">Historic accidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Historic accidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_accidents_by_country" title="Nuclear power accidents by country" class="mw-redirect">Nuclear power accidents by country</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/220px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/330px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/440px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1095" data-file-height="612"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> The 2011 
        <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a> in Japan, the worst 
        <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_accident" title="Nuclear accident" class="mw-redirect">nuclear accident</a> 
        <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">in 25 years</a>, displaced 50,000 households after 
        <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> leaked into the air, soil and sea.
        <sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Radiation checks led to bans of some shipments of vegetables and fish.
        <sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The nuclear industry says that new technology and oversight have made nuclear plants much safer, but <a href="/wiki/International_Nuclear_Event_Scale" title="International Nuclear Event Scale">57 small accidents</a> have occurred since the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a> in 1986 until 2008. Two thirds of these mishaps occurred in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-sovsing_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sovsing-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> The French Atomic Energy Agency (CEA) has concluded that technical innovation cannot eliminate the risk of human errors in nuclear plant operation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>According to <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Sovacool" title="Benjamin Sovacool" class="mw-redirect">Benjamin Sovacool</a>, an interdisciplinary team from MIT in 2003 estimated that given the expected growth of nuclear power from 2005 – 2055, at least four serious nuclear accidents would be expected in that period.<sup id="cite_ref-sovsing_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sovsing-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> However the MIT study does not take into account improvements in safety since 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-uvm.edu_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uvm.edu-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology_2003_49_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology_2003_49-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flexibility_of_nuclear_power_plants">Flexibility of nuclear power plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Flexibility of nuclear power plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>Nuclear stations are used primarily for base load because of economic considerations. The fuel cost of operations for a nuclear plant is smaller than the fuel cost for operation of coal or gas plants. There is no cost saving if you run a nuclear plant at less than full capacity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>However, nuclear plants are routinely used in load following mode on a large scale in France, although "it is generally accepted that this is not an ideal economic situation for nuclear plants."<sup id="cite_ref-nei_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nei-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> Unit&nbsp;A at the German <a href="/wiki/Biblis_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Biblis Nuclear Power Plant">Biblis Nuclear Power Plant</a> is designed to in- and decrease its output 15% per minute between 40 and 100% of its nominal power.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> Boiling water reactors normally have load-following capability, implemented by varying the recirculation water flow.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_power_plants">Future power plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Future power plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
