     <p>A new generation of designs for nuclear power plants, known as the <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">Generation IV reactors</a>, are the subject of active research. Many of these new designs specifically attempt to make fission reactors cleaner, safer and/or less of a risk to the proliferation of nuclear weapons. <a href="/wiki/Passive_nuclear_safety" title="Passive nuclear safety">Passively safe</a> plants (such as the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Simplified_Boiling_Water_Reactor" title="Economic Simplified Boiling Water Reactor">ESBWR</a>) are available to be built<sup id="cite_ref-ANSESBWR_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANSESBWR-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> and other reactors that are designed to be nearly fool-proof are being pursued.<sup id="cite_ref-TIMESAFE_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TIMESAFE-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">Fusion reactors</a>, which are still in the early stages of development, diminish or eliminate some of the risks associated with nuclear fission.<sup id="cite_ref-PWFUSION_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PWFUSION-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Two 1600 <a href="/wiki/MWe" title="MWe" class="mw-redirect">MWe</a> <a href="/wiki/EPR_(nuclear_reactor)" title="EPR (nuclear reactor)">European Pressurized Reactors</a> (EPRs) are being built in Europe, and two are being built in China. The reactors are a joint effort of French <a href="/wiki/AREVA" title="AREVA" class="mw-redirect">AREVA</a> and German <a href="/wiki/Siemens_AG" title="Siemens AG" class="mw-redirect">Siemens AG</a>, and will be the largest reactors in the world. One EPR is in <a href="/wiki/Olkiluoto" title="Olkiluoto" class="mw-redirect">Olkiluoto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Olkiluoto_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Olkiluoto Nuclear Power Plant">Olkiluoto Nuclear Power Plant</a>. The reactor was originally scheduled to go online in 2009, but has been repeatedly delayed,<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> and as of September 2014 has been pushed back to 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Preparatory work for the EPR at the <a href="/wiki/Flamanville_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Flamanville Nuclear Power Plant">Flamanville Nuclear Power Plant</a> in <a href="/wiki/Flamanville,_Manche" title="Flamanville, Manche">Flamanville, Manche</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> was started in 2006, with a scheduled completion date of 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> The French reactor has also been delayed, and was projected, in 2013, to launch in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> The two Chinese EPRs are part of the <a href="/wiki/Taishan_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Taishan Nuclear Power Plant">Taishan Nuclear Power Plant</a> in <a href="/wiki/Taishan" title="Taishan">Taishan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>. The Taishan reactors were scheduled to go online in 2014 and 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> but that has been delayed to 2015 and 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>As of March 2007<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there are seven nuclear power plants under construction in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and five in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In November 2011 Gulf Power stated that by the end of 2012 it hopes to finish buying off 4000 acres of land north of Pensacola, Florida in order to build a possible nuclear power plant.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2010 Russia launched a <a href="/wiki/Russian_floating_nuclear_power_station" title="Russian floating nuclear power station">floating nuclear power plant</a>. The £100 million vessel, the <a href="/wiki/Akademik_Lomonosov" title="Akademik Lomonosov">Akademik Lomonosov</a>, is the first of seven plants that will bring vital energy resources to remote Russian regions.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>By 2025, <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> nations would have a total of 29 nuclear power plants, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> will have 4 nuclear power plants, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> 4, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> 5 and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> 16 from nothing at all in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2013 China had 32<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> nuclear reactors under construction, the highest number in the world.</p> 
     <p>Expansion at two Nuclear Power Plants in the United States, <a href="/wiki/Vogtle_Electric_Generating_Plant" title="Vogtle Electric Generating Plant">Plant Vogtle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virgil_C._Summer_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Virgil C. Summer Nuclear Generating Station">V. C. Summer Nuclear Power Plant</a>, located in Georgia and South Carolina, respectively, are scheduled to be completed between 2016 and 2019. The two new Plant Vogtle reactors, and the two new reactors at Virgil C. Summer Nuclear Plant, represent the first nuclear power construction projects in the United States since the <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island" title="Three Mile Island" class="mw-redirect">Three Mile Island</a> nuclear accident in 1979.</p> 
     <p>Several countries have begun <a href="/wiki/Thorium-based_nuclear_power" title="Thorium-based nuclear power">Thorium-based nuclear power</a> programs. <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">Thorium</a> is four times more abundant within nature than uranium. Over 60% of thorium's ore <a href="/wiki/Monazite" title="Monazite">monazite</a> is found in five countries: Australia, the United States, India, Brazil, and Norway. These thorium resources are enough to power current energy needs for thousands of years.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Thorium_fuel_cycle" title="Thorium fuel cycle">thorium fuel cycle</a> is able to generate nuclear energy with a lower output of radiotoxic waste than the uranium fuel cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="noprint portal tright" style="border:solid #aaa 1px;margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em">

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_power_plant&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;"> 
      <ol class="references"> 
       <li id="cite_note-iaea.org-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-iaea.org_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.iaea.org/pris/">"PRIS - Home"</a>.        <li id="cite_note-UIC-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-UIC_2-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//web.archive.org/web/20080303234143/http://www.uic.com.au/reactors.htm">"World Nuclear Power Reactors 2007-08 and Uranium Requirements"</a>.        <li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.world-nuclear.org/info/inf02.html">World Nuclear Association; The economics of nuclear Power, updated July 2012</a> Their operations and maintenance (O&amp;M) and fuel costs (including used fuel management) are, along with hydropower plants, at the low end of the spectrum and make them very suitable as base-load power suppliers.</span></li>
